The Ninth Objection: Effect as Necessary Concomitant
Al-Razi begins his answer to the ninth objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ninth objection about necessary concomitance and the beginning of Al-Razi's response, including the implication that the absence of an effect may not always indicate the absence of its cause.

Core Idea
The absence of an effect does not always prove the absence of its cause.

In the ongoing discussion of causality, Al-Razi addresses an objection that challenges the relationship between cause and effect. The objectors claim that if an effect necessarily follows its cause, then the effect's absence must indicate the cause's absence. Al-Razi's reply, though incomplete in our text, begins to qualify this notion by suggesting other possibilities. This lesson explores the objection and the initial part of his answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the claim made by the objectors: that if the effect is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the cause, then the absence of the effect indicates the absence of the cause.
The objection relies on a strict correlation between cause and effect.
2
Examine Al-Razi's initial response
Al-Razi begins his answer with 'it indicates either the absence of the cause...' but the text cuts off. Notice that he does not accept the objection entirely; he implies there are multiple possibilities.
The full answer likely continues with 'or the absence of the condition, or the presence of a hindrance.'
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why Al-Razi might say that the absence of an effect could be due to factors other than the absence of its cause. This preserves the possibility of causal efficacy while allowing for additional conditions.

As for the ninth objection: it is their saying: If the effect were a necessary concomitant of the cause, then the absence of the effect would indicate the absence of the cause. Its answer: it indicates either the absence of the cause,

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the answer to the ninth objection. The objection states that if the effect is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the cause, then if the effect is absent, it must indicate the absence of the cause. Al-Razi starts his answer: 'it indicates either the absence of the cause...' but the text breaks off. The answer is incomplete. The full answer likely continues with 'or the absence of the condition or the presence of a hindrance.' Since we only have this fragment, we include it as is.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an effect is a necessary consequence of a cause, then the absence of the effect always proves the absence of the cause.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the absence of an effect could indicate the absence of a condition or the presence of a hindrance, not necessarily the absence of the cause itself.

Checkpoint

B) The absence of the effect indicates the absence of the cause.
C) The effect is not necessary for the cause.

Key Takeaway
The absence of an effect does not necessarily prove the absence of its cause; other factors like missing conditions or hindrances may be at play.

The Eighth Objection: Angelic Agency vs. Formative Power
Answer to eighth objection and critique of Aristotelian view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author answers the eighth objection by invoking an angelic free agent and why he rejects the Aristotelian view of an inherent formative power.

Core Idea
Angelic agency resolves the problem of organ differentiation without natural causes.

The eighth objection questions how different organs can arise from a uniform substance like sperm. The author offers a straightforward answer: God appoints an angel to shape the organs according to wisdom. This response sidesteps the need for natural causality. The author then contrasts this with the Aristotelian explanation of a 'formative power' and dismisses it as highly improbable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a rhetorical question structure: 'لم لا يجوز أن يقال...'
2
Identify the objection and the answer
The objection is the generation of different organs from homogeneous fluid. Locate the author's answer: he invokes an angel that God appoints to form the organs.
The angel is described as 'فاعل مختار' (free agent).
3
Understand the role of the angelic agent
Consider why calling the angel a free agent matters. It means the angel acts by choice, not by nature, which removes the need for a natural inherent cause.
This explanation makes the objection moot: the angel produces differentiation according to God's wisdom.
4
Contrast with the Aristotelian view
The author mentions that followers of Aristotle attribute formation to 'القوة المصورة' (formative power) in the fluid. He dismisses this view as highly unlikely, referencing his own work.
This contrast shows the author's preference for a voluntary divine agent over a natural inherent power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النقض الثامن: وهو تولد الأعضاء المختلفة من النطفة المتشابهة الأجزاء. فنقول: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن الله تعالى ملكاً تلك يصور من النطقة تلك الأعضاء المختلفة، بحسب المصلحة والحكمة. وذلك الملك فاعل مختار، وعلى هذا التقدير يسقط السؤال. وأما أصحاب أرسطاطاليس فقد اتفقوا على إسناد تصوير الأعضاء إلى القوة المصورة الحالة في جرم النطفة. ولا شك أن القول به في غاية البعد، على ما قررناه في كتاب «القوى الطبيعية» من علم النفس.
As for the eighth objection: it is the generation of different organs from the homogeneous parts of the sperm/fluid. So we say: Why is it not permissible to say that God—exalted is He—has an angel who forms from the sperm those different organs, according to benefit and wisdom? And that angel is an agent with free choice, and on this assumption the question falls. As for the followers of Aristotle, they have agreed on attributing the formation of organs to the formative power inherent in the substance of the sperm. And there is no doubt that this view is extremely unlikely, as we have established in the book 'The Natural Powers' from the science of the soul (psychology).
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth objection and the author's response. The objection states that different organs (e.g., heart, liver) arise from a homogeneous fluid (النطفة), which seems to require differentiation without a cause. The author answers by suggesting that God appoints an angel to form the organs, and that angel is a free agent (فاعل مختار). This explanation removes the need for a natural inherent cause. The author then contrasts this with the Aristotelian view, which attributes formation to a 'formative power' (القوة المصورة) residing in the fluid. He dismisses that view as extremely unlikely, referring to his previous work on natural powers in psychology.

Key Terms
النطفة (sperm/fluid)
القوة المصورة (formative power)
فاعل مختار (free agent)
أرسطاطاليس (Aristotle)

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the eighth objection regarding the generation of organs?
A) He invokes a natural inherent power.
B) He posits an angelic agent with free will.
C) He denies the phenomenon.
D) He argues it is impossible to answer.

Key Takeaway
The author resolves the eighth objection by invoking an angelic free agent, and he dismisses the Aristotelian notion of an inherent formative power as highly unlikely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What specific thought can you adopt today to turn your reflection into worship?

How would your trust in Allah increase if you saw His hand in everything?
?
The passage says the angel acts 'according to benefit and wisdom.' Do you live with the conviction that every detail of your existence is crafted with perfect wisdom by Allah's command, even when you don't understand it? What would change if you truly believed that?

The Ninth Objection: Effect as a Necessary Concomitant of the Cause
The author answers the ninth objection by distinguishing between removal of cause and removal of a condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the eternity of the cause by distinguishing between the removal of the cause and the removal of a condition when the effect is absent.

Core Idea
Absence of an effect may point to a missing condition, not a missing cause.

The ninth objection argues that if the effect is a necessary concomitant of the cause, then the absence of the effect would require the absence of the cause, threatening the cause's eternity. The author's answer is a careful logical distinction: the absence indicates either the cause's absence or the absence of a condition necessary for its influence. This preserves the cause's eternity while explaining temporal effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice how the objection is a logical conditional: if effect is necessary concomitant, then its absence implies cause's absence.
2
Identify the two possibilities
In the answer, the author lists two possibilities for what the absence of the effect might indicate. Find them and note the key Arabic terms: 'ارتفاع العلة' and 'ارتفاع ما كان شرطاً'.
The two possibilities are: (1) absence of cause, (2) absence of a condition.
3
Understand the role of the condition
The author adds that the condition is 'حادث آخر قبله' (another prior event). Reflect on why this is important: a condition is separate from the cause, so the cause can be eternal even if its effect is not yet present.
The condition is a prior created thing, not the cause itself.
4
Reflect on the defence of eternity
Consider how this answer protects the doctrine that the cause (God) is eternal. The absence of the world does not prove God's absence; it only shows that a condition for creation was missing. This allows for a temporal world from an eternal cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النقض التاسع: وهو قولهم: لو كان المعلول من لوازم العلة، لكان ارتفاع المعلول يدل على ارتفاع العلة. فجوابه: إنه يدل إما على ارتفاع العلة، أو على ارتفاع ما كان شرطاً لتأثير العلة، في جانب المعلول. وذلك الشرط حادث آخر قبله.
As for the ninth objection: it is their saying: If the effect were a necessary concomitant of the cause, then the absence of the effect would indicate the absence of the cause. So its answer is: It indicates either the absence of the cause, or the absence of what was a condition for the cause's influence on the side of the effect. And that condition is another prior event.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth objection and its concise answer. The objection is a logical argument: if the effect (معلول) is a necessary concomitant (لازم) of the cause (علة), then whenever the effect is absent, the cause must also be absent. But we observe effects (like the world) that are absent; this would imply the cause is absent, contradicting the eternity of the cause. The author's answer distinguishes two possibilities: the absence indicates either (1) the cause's absence, or (2) the absence of a condition (شرط) necessary for the cause's influence. That condition is a prior event (حادث آخر قبله), so the cause may be eternal but its effect is delayed due to a condition. This preserves the cause's eternity while explaining the temporal appearance of effects.

Key Terms
المعلول (effect)
العلة (cause)
لازم (necessary concomitant)
شرط (condition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the answer implies a change in the cause itself when the effect is absent.
Correction: The author says the absence indicates either the cause's absence or a missing condition, not a change in the cause. The cause remains eternal and unchanged.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the ninth objection, what does the absence of an effect indicate?
A) Only the absence of the cause.
B) Either the absence of the cause or the absence of a condition for the cause's influence.
C) That the cause is not eternal.
D) That the effect never existed.

Key Takeaway
The absence of an effect may be due to the absence of a condition, not the absence of the cause, preserving the cause's eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your dua seems unanswered, do you immediately doubt that Allah is the ultimate cause, or do you stop to consider that perhaps a condition (like sincerity or timing) was missing? How can this lesson change your reaction to delayed answers?
?
Think of a specific failure or disappointment in your life right now. According to this lesson, is it possible that the cause (Allah's will) is present, but a condition you neglected is absent? What condition might that be, and what will you do about it starting today?
?
If you were to truly internalize that every effect requires not just a cause but also a condition, how would that affect your patience when striving for a goal? Will you persist in seeking the conditions rather than despairing of the cause? Commit to one condition you will actively work to fulfill this week.

The Tenth Objection: Causality as Non-Additional Attribute
The author refutes the tenth objection by arguing causality is not an additional attribute and cites earlier debates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosopher's objection about daily events fails because causality is not an additional attribute, and how the argument avoids infinite regress.

Core Idea
Causality is not an extra attribute; it is just the relation itself.

The tenth objection claims that if an eternal cause produces a temporal effect, it must acquire a new property of causality, which would itself need a cause. This passage presents the author's crucial answer: causality is not an additional attribute over the cause's essence. Understanding this distinction is key to defending God's causality without change.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the objection about daily events and the author's initial answer about conditioning.
The objection is that the eternal cause seems to change when it produces a temporal effect.
2
Focus on the refined objection
The objector then says causality itself is a temporal attribute requiring another cause. Notice how the author restates this.
3
Understand the core argument
The author argues that causality is not an additional attribute (وصف زائد) over the essence of the cause and effect. If it were, it would need a cause, leading to infinite regress.
This is the key point: causality is intrinsic to the relation, not a separate quality.
4
Note the reference to earlier discussions
The author mentions that deeper investigations on this topic have already passed in the chapter on establishing the Necessary of Existence.
This connects the argument to broader theological foundations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النقض العاشر: وهو النقض بالحوادث اليومية. فجوابه: ما تقدم أن العلة المؤثرة أزلية، عامة الفيض. إلا أن صدور كل حادث معين عن تلك العلة الأزلية، مشروط بذلك الحادث الذي كان سابقاً عليه. أما قوله: إن ذلك القديم ما كان علة لهذا المعلول الحادث، ثم صار علة له، فهذه العلة صفة حادثة، فلا بد لها من مؤثر، فنقول: الذي يمكن أن يذكر في دفع هذا السؤال: إن علية الشيء لغيره، لا يمكن أن يكون وصفاً زائداً على ذات العلة. وذات المعلول، وإلا فذلك الزائد، وصف محتاج إلى الغير، فيكون ممكناً لذاته، فيفتقر إلى علة، فتكون علية العلة لتلك العلية زائدة عليها، ولزم التسلسل. فهذا أقصى ما يمكن أن يقال في دفع هذا السؤال. وقد مضى في باب إثبات واجب الوجوب: ابحاث عميقة في أن كون الشيء علة لغيره هل هو وصف زائد على ذات العلة أم لا؟
As for the tenth objection: it is the objection concerning daily events. So its answer: That which preceded, that the effective cause is eternal, universal in emanation. But the issuance of each particular event from that eternal cause is conditioned by that event which preceded it. As for his saying: 'That eternal [cause] was not a cause for this temporal effect, then it became a cause for it, so this causality is a temporal attribute, so it must have an effect (another cause).' So we say: What can be mentioned in repelling this question: That the causality (علية) of a thing for something else cannot be an attribute additional to the essence of the cause and the essence of the effect; otherwise, that additional [attribute] is an attribute in need of another, so it would be possible in itself, thus it would require a cause, so the causality of the cause for that causality would be additional to it, and infinite regress would follow. So this is the utmost that can be said in repelling this question. And there have already passed in the chapter on establishing the Necessary of Existence profound investigations concerning whether a thing's being a cause for something else is an additional attribute to the essence of the cause or not.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth objection and a detailed answer. The objection concerns daily events (الحوادث اليومية) — why some events occur now and not earlier if the cause is eternal? The initial answer is that the eternal cause's emanation is conditioned by prior events. The objection then shifts: if the eternal cause was not a cause for a particular temporal effect and then becomes a cause, that implies a change in the cause, which would be a temporal attribute requiring another cause. To refute this, the author argues that causality (علية) is not an additional attribute (وصف زائد) over the essence of the cause and the essence of the effect. If it were, it would be a possible attribute needing a cause, leading to infinite regress (تسلسل). The author notes that this issue has been deeply investigated in the chapter on establishing the Necessary of Existence, referencing earlier discussions.

Key Terms
العلة المؤثرة (effective cause)
عامة الفيض (universal emanation)
علية (causality)
وصف زائد (additional attribute)
التسلسل (infinite regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the answer implies the cause changes or acquires a new property.
Correction: The author argues that causality is not a real attribute that changes; it is merely the relation between cause and effect, not an additional quality.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what would happen if causality were an additional attribute over the essence of the cause?
A) It would be eternal.
B) It would require a cause and lead to infinite regress.
C) It would be identical to the cause.
D) It would prove the world's eternity.

Key Takeaway
Causality is not an additional attribute over the cause's essence, so the eternal cause does not change when producing temporal effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you learned that causality is not an additional attribute in the cause. This means that when you act, your action does not introduce a new attribute in you that changes your essence. But do you live as if your actions define who you are? Are you afraid that a past failure has added a permanent stain to your soul? How would your view of yourself change if you truly believed that no act can add an attribute to your essence — that you are not defined by your deeds, but by your relationship with Allah? What would you need to let go of to live in that freedom?
?
The author argues that if causality were an additional attribute, it would lead to an infinite regress. In your daily life, are there things you rely on as independent causes — your intelligence, your wealth, your status? Do you treat these as if they have causal power in themselves, separate from Allah? Imagine stripping away every secondary cause and attributing all causality directly to Allah. Does that thought make you feel vulnerable or secure? What is one area where you can practice this reliance today, even in a small way?
?
The objection raised in this lesson points to a deep anxiety: if God's causality is eternal, why does the effect happen now and not earlier? This question touches on the mystery of divine timing. Are you impatient with Allah's timing in your life? Do you find yourself thinking that if Allah were really the cause, things should have happened sooner or differently? Reflect on a specific situation where you are waiting. Can you surrender your timeline to the eternal cause, trusting that His universal emanation is conditioned by a wisdom you do not see? What would it look like to actively wait with trust instead of frustration?

Critique of the Philosophers' Defense of the Eternity of Bodies
The author critiques the philosophers' defense of the eternity of bodies using a conditional argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author turns the philosophers' own logic against them to argue against the eternity of bodies, even if their excuse about the formation of the world were accepted.

Core Idea
Even if the philosophers' excuse were valid, their proof for eternity fails.

In this passage, the author delivers a powerful rebuttal. He does not even need to disprove the philosophers' excuse about the world's formation differing from daily events. Instead, he shows that even accepting that excuse, their argument for the eternity of bodies collapses. By proposing a scenario where an eternal intellect or soul serves as a locus for events, he undermines the necessity of the world's eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'لو صح واستقام، فإنه لا يمكنهم...'.
2
Identify the philosophers' excuse
The passage refers to an excuse the philosophers gave for distinguishing the formation of the entire corporeal world from daily events. Try to infer what this excuse might be from context.
The excuse likely involves the idea that the world's formation requires a prior event, while daily events do not.
3
Understand the author's conditional argument
The author says: even if the excuse were valid, the philosophers still cannot use their proof for eternity. Understand that this is a concession for the sake of argument.
This is a powerful rhetorical move: assuming their premise does not save their conclusion.
4
Analyze the counter-argument
The author presents a hypothetical objection: since the philosophers accept that the eternal cause produces temporal effects conditioned by prior events, why can't one say the whole body of the world is temporal, with an eternal intellect or soul as a locus?
This turns the philosophers' logic against them by showing their own principles allow for a created world.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this counter-argument undermines the philosophers' proof for eternity. It shows that the necessity of a prior event does not require the world to be eternal.
This is the key insight: the philosophers' own conditional reasoning can be used to support the temporal creation of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم: أن هذا العذر الذي ذكره الفلاسفة في الفرق بين تكوين كل العالم الجسماني، وبين تكوين الحوادث اليومية. لو صح واستقام، فإنه لا يمكنهم مع ذلك أن يحتجوا بالحجة التي ذكروها على قدم الأجسام، وقدم السموات والأرضين. فإن لقائل أن يقول: لما جوزتم أن تلك العلة القديمة يصدع عنها المعلولات المحدثة، لأجل أن كل حادث مسبوق بحادث آخر وكان حصول الحادث المتقدم، شرطاً لفيضان الحادث المتأخر عن تلك العلة القديمة فلم لا يجوز أيضاً أن يقال: جملة الأجسام، حادثة، ولها أول ومبدأ، إلا أنه كان قد وجد في الأزل عقل أو نفس، وكان ذلك الجوهر المجرد محلاً للحوادث
And know: that this excuse which the philosophers mentioned regarding the distinction between the formation of the entire corporeal world and the formation of daily events, if it were sound and correct, still they cannot with that argue by the proof they mentioned for the eternity of bodies and the eternity of the heavens and the earths. For an objector may say: Since you have permitted that that eternal cause emanates temporal effects because every event is preceded by another event, and the occurrence of the prior event is a condition for the emanation of the later event from that eternal cause, then why may it not also be said: The totality of bodies is temporal, having a beginning and a start, except that there had existed from eternity an intellect or a soul, and that abstract substance was a locus for events...
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents a final critique of the philosophers' position. The author follows up the answers to the ten objections by arguing that even if the philosophers' excuse (that the world's formation differs from daily events) were valid, they still cannot use their proof for the eternity of bodies and heavens. The author presents a hypothetical counter-argument: since the philosophers accept that the eternal cause produces temporal effects conditioned by prior events, why can't one say that the entire body of the world is temporal, having a beginning, but there existed from eternity an intellect or soul that served as a locus for events? This undermines the philosophers' claim that the world must be eternal because it cannot be preceded by another event. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the gist is clear: the philosophers' own logic can be turned against them to allow for a created world.

Key Terms
العذر (excuse)
الفلاسفة (philosophers)
تكوين العالم (formation of the world)
قدم الأجسام (eternity of bodies)
عقل (intellect)
نفس (soul)
جوهر مجرد (abstract substance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the author accepts the philosophers' excuse and concedes that the world is eternal.
Correction: The author actually argues that even if the excuse were accepted, it still does not prove the eternity of bodies; he turns the argument against the philosophers.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue about the philosophers' excuse regarding the distinction between the formation of the world and daily events?
A) He accepts it fully.
B) He argues that even if valid, it does not prove the eternity of bodies.
C) He dismisses it as invalid.
D) He uses it to prove the world's eternity.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' own logic can be turned against them to allow for the creation of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers were convinced their proof was unassailable, yet the author exposed its hidden flaw using their own logic. Is there a way you have been defending a belief—perhaps about your own character or your relationship with Allah—that you have never truly questioned? What would it mean to examine that defense as rigorously as the author examines the philosophers' proof?
?
Consider the possibility that some of your most cherished arguments for your faith might have weaknesses you've never admitted. Are you willing to seek out those weaknesses, even if it feels uncomfortable? What might Allah reveal to you through that honesty?

intermediate
6 min
The Nature of Causality and the Tenth Objection
Answering the tenth objection and analyzing causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the tenth objection regarding daily events and the argument that causality is not an additional attribute on the essence of the cause.

Core Idea
Causality is a relation, not an extra attribute.

In this passage, the author addresses a classic objection: if the eternal cause becomes a cause for a new effect, does that imply a change in God? The response is a deep insight into the nature of causality, arguing that it is not an added attribute but a relation between the cause and effect. Understanding this is crucial for avoiding infinite regress in theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the tenth objection
Read the beginning: the objection concerns daily events. Understand the challenge: if the eternal cause is unchanging, how can it be the cause of new temporal effects?
This sets up the problem.
2
Review the initial answer
The author states that the eternal cause is always effective, but each specific effect is conditioned by prior events. This is a key concept.
3
Analyze the counter-argument
The opponent argues that if causality were an attribute that appears in the cause when it produces a new effect, then that attribute would be temporal and require another cause.
This is the crux of the objection.
4
Understand the refutation
The author responds that causality is not an additional attribute on the essence of the cause, but a relation between the cause and effect. Otherwise, infinite regress follows.
This is the core argument.
5
Connect to broader theology
Note the reference to earlier discussions on the Necessarily Existent. This argument is part of a larger effort to defend God's immutability.

وأما النقض العاشر : وهو النقض بالحوادث اليومية . فجوابه : ما تقدم أن العلة المؤثرة أزلية ، عامة الفيض . إلا أن صدور كل حادث معين عن تلك العلة الأزلية ، مشروط بذلك الحادث الذي كان سابقاً عليه . أما قوله : ه إن ذلك القديم ما كان علة لهذا المعلول الحادث، ثم صار علة له ، فهذه العلة صفة حادثة ، فلا بد لها من مؤثر ، فنقول : الذي يمكن أن يذكر في دفع هذا السؤال : إن علية الشيء لغيره ، لا يمكن أن يكون وصفاً زائداً على ذات العلة . وذات المعلول ، وإلا فذلك الزائد ، وصف محتاج إلى الغير ، فيكون ممكناً لذاته ، فيفتقر إلى علة ، فتكون علية العلة لتلك العلية زائدة عليها ، ولزم التسلسل . فهذا أقصى ما يمكن أن يقال في دفع هذا السؤال . وقد مضى في باب إثبات واجب الوجوب : ابحاث عميقة في أن كون الشيء علة [لغيره] هل هو وصف زائد على ذات العلة [أم لا] ؟
As for the tenth objection: it is the objection concerning daily events. Its answer: what has preceded, that the effective cause is eternal, general in emanation. However, the occurrence of each specific temporal event from that eternal cause is conditioned by that event which was prior to it. As for his statement: 'That eternal was not a cause for this temporal effect, then became a cause for it, so this causality is an accidental attribute, so it must have an influencer,' we say: that which can be mentioned in repelling this question is: that the causality of a thing for something else cannot be an attribute additional to the essence of the cause and the essence of the effect. Otherwise, that additional attribute is an attribute needing another, so it would be contingent by itself, thus it would need a cause, so the causality of the cause for that causality would be additional to it, and infinite regress would follow. So this is the utmost that can be said in repelling this question. And it has passed in the chapter on establishing the Necessarily Existent profound investigations concerning whether a thing's being a cause for something else is an attribute additional to the essence of the cause or not.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by a deeper analysis of causality. The author begins by stating the tenth objection: it concerns daily temporal events. The answer reiterates that the eternal cause is always effective but conditioned by prior events. Then the author addresses a specific counter-argument: that if the eternal cause was not originally causing a particular effect and then becomes a cause, that would imply a new attribute of causality in the cause, which would itself be temporal and require another cause. The author's response is to deny that causality is an additional attribute over and above the essence of the cause and the effect, for if it were, it would be contingent and lead to infinite regress. This is a typical kalām argument against accidental attributes in the divine essence. The author ends by referencing earlier discussions in the chapter on the Necessarily Existent about whether causality is an additional attribute.

Refuting the Philosophers' Argument for the Eternity of Bodies
The author presents a counterargument that turns the philosophers' own principle against them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the philosophers' own principle of temporal conditioning to refute their claim that the corporeal world is eternal.

Core Idea
The philosophers' own logic can be turned against them to prove the world's temporal origin.

The author engages deeply with the philosophers' reasoning. He grants their distinction between the formation of the entire world and daily events but shows that it leads to a counterexample that actually demonstrates the world's temporality. This passage illustrates a powerful dialectical strategy: accepting a premise and deriving a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal translation, noting the structure of the objection.
Notice how the author first states the philosophers' excuse, then presents the counterargument.
2
Identify the philosophers' principle
The philosophers claim that every temporal event is preceded by another event and that the prior event is a condition for the later. Understand this principle.
This principle is key to the refutation.
3
Trace the counterargument
The author posits an eternal intellect with an infinite series of perceptions. A specific perception then conditions the creation of the corporeal world, making it temporal despite the infinite chain.
This shows that the same principle can be used to argue for temporality.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the philosophers' proof fails because this possibility cannot be dismissed. Consider why this is a strong refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن هذا العذر الذي ذكره الفلاسفة في الفرق بين تكوين كل العالم الجسماني ، وبين تكوين الحوادث اليومية . لو صح واستقام ، فإنه لا يمكنهم مع ذلك أن يحتجوا بالحجة التي ذكروها على قدم الأجسام، وقدم السموات والأرضين . فإن لقائل أن يقول : لما جوزتم أن تلك العلة القديمة يصدع عنها المعلولات المحدثة ، لأجل أن كل حادث مسبوق بحادث [آخر] وكان حصول الحادث المتقدم، شرطاً لفيضان الحادث المتأخر عن تلك العلة القديمة فلم لا يجوز أيضاً أن يقال : جملة الأجسام،حادثة، ولها أول ومبدأ، إلا أنه كان قد وجد في الأزل عقل أو نفس ، وكان ذلك الجوهر المجرد محلا للحوادث النفسانية والتغيرات الادراكية ، وكان كل واحد منها مسبوقاً بآخر ، لا إلى أول ، ثم لما انتهت تلك التصورات المتعاقبة ، والادراكات المتلاحقة في جانب لا يزال إلى تصور خاص ، وتعقل خاص ، وكان حدوث ذلك التصور شرطاً لفيضان وجود العالم الجسماني عن العلة القديمة [فلا جرم حدث العالم الجسماني عن العلة القديمة] في ذلك الوقت . وإذا كان هذا الاحتمال قائماً ، فحينئذ لا يمكن الاستدلال بالحجة المذكورة على كون العالم الجسماني : قديماً ؟ فهذا سؤال واقع ولا يكاد يمكن دفعه . وهذا آخر الكلام في تقرير هذه الحجة . ومن الله التوفيق [والإرشاد]
And know: that this excuse which the philosophers mentioned to distinguish between the formation of the entire corporeal world and the formation of daily events — if it were correct and sound, they still cannot with that argue using the proof they mentioned for the eternity of bodies and the eternity of the heavens and earths. For an objector can say: since you allowed that the eternal cause emanates temporal effects because every temporal event is preceded by another event, and the occurrence of the prior event is a condition for the emanation of the subsequent event from that eternal cause, then why is it not also permissible to say: the entirety of bodies is temporal, having a beginning and origin, except that there existed in eternity an intellect or a soul, and that disembodied essence was a locus for psychic events and perceptual changes, each one preceded by another without a first, then when those successive perceptions and consecutive cognitions ended at a specific perception and specific intellection, and the occurrence of that perception was a condition for the emanation of the existence of the corporeal world from the eternal cause, so then the corporeal world occurred from the eternal cause at that time. And if this possibility stands, then the mentioned proof cannot be used to prove that the corporeal world is eternal. So this is a real objection that can hardly be repelled. And this is the end of the discussion on establishing this argument. And from Allah is success and guidance.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' argument for the eternity of the corporeal world. The author first states that even if the philosophers' distinction between the formation of the entire world and daily events were accepted, they still cannot use that to prove the eternity of bodies. The author then presents a counterargument: the same principle that every temporal event is preceded by another and conditioned by it can be applied to the entire corporeal world. One can posit an eternal intellect or soul that undergoes an infinite series of psychic changes, and a specific perception at a particular time conditions the emanation of the corporeal world from the eternal cause. This makes the world temporally originated despite the infinite chain of prior events. Since this possibility exists, the philosophers' proof fails. The author concludes that this objection is difficult to refute, thus ending the discussion.

Acknowledging a Weakness in the Proof for the World's Eternity
Author concludes a proof by acknowledging a possible objection that invalidates it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a possible sequence of perceptions exposes a flaw in the argument for the eternity of the corporeal world.

Core Idea
Even the best proofs can be undone by a possible objection.

Here, the author concludes a discussion of a proof for the world's eternity by honestly acknowledging a devastating objection. The objection involves an infinite chain of perceptions that eventually culminates in a specific thought that becomes the condition for the world's creation. This shows the proof cannot rule out a temporal origin.

Guided Path

The passage is dense; take your time.
2

The key terms are تصور, تعقل, فيضان, علة قديمة.
3
Understand the implication
Ask yourself: if this scenario is possible, does the proof still show the world is eternal? The author says no, and admits this is a real difficulty.
The author is not asserting this scenario is true, only that it is a possible counterexample.
4
Reflect on the author's honesty
Notice that the author ends by stating this is a real question that cannot be easily refuted, and seeks divine help. This shows intellectual integrity.

Argument from the Relational Nature of Maker and Made
Author presents a proof for the world's eternity using the category of relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the logical relationship between 'maker' and 'made' leads to an argument for the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
If the world were created, God would change from potential to actual maker—impossible.

The author presents a sophisticated argument rooted in the category of relation. By analyzing the terms 'maker' and 'made' as correlatives, he shows that if the world were temporal, God's attribute of being a maker would shift from potentiality to actuality—a change that would require an external cause. This leads to the conclusion that the world must be eternal to preserve divine immutability.

Guided Path
1
Understand the relational terms
Recognize that 'maker' and 'made' are relative (مضافان). They exist together either potentially or actually.
This is the foundation of the argument.
2
Assume the world is not eternal
Follow the reductio: suppose the world is temporally originated (محدث). Then in pre-eternity, the made (world) exists only potentially.
3
Trace the implication for the maker
If the made is potential, the maker is potential. When the world becomes actual, the maker becomes actual. This transition is a change in the maker.
The argument claims this change is problematic.
4
Analyze the need for a cause
Anything that moves from potentiality to actuality requires an external mover. If the maker changed, something else would have caused it, leading to infinite regress or contradiction.
5
Conclude the eternity of the world
The only way to avoid change in the maker is that the made (world) always existed actually, i.e., the world is eternal.
The conclusion is not fully in the text but is implied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني
في تقرير وجوه أخرى من الحلائل متفرعة على فاعلية المبدأ الأول
اعلم أن للقوم أن يقولوا : الصانع والفاعل من مقولة المضاف . فالصانع مقول بالقياس إلى المصنوع ، كما أن المصنوع مقول بالقياس إلى الصانع ، والمضافان موجودان معاً. فإن كان المصنوع موجوداً بالقوة ، كان الصانع صانعاً بالقوة ، وإن كان المصنوع موجوداً بالفعل ، كان الصانع صانعاً بالفعل
وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : لو لم يكن العالم أزلياً ، لما كان المصنوع موجوداً بالفعل في الأزل بل بالقوة . ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لما كان الصانع صانعاً بالفعل في الأزل ، بل بالقوة . ثم إذا وجد فيها لا يزال المصنوع فقد صار الصانع فيما لا يزال صانعاً بالفعل. فيثبت : أن العالم لو كان محدثاً لزم أن يقال : الصانع كان صانعاً بالقوة ، ثم صار صانعاً بالفعل . وكل ما كان بالقوة ثم صار بالفعل، فانتقاله من القوة إلى الفعل ، ومن العدم إلى الوجود لا يكون لذاته ، وإلا لكان بالفعل أبداً . بل لا بد وأن يكون ذلك الانتقال لأجل أن غيره نقله من القوة إلى الفعل ، فلو كان العالم محدثاً ، لاحتاج الصانع إلى شيء ينقله من كونه صانعاً بالقوة إلى كونه صانعاً بالفعل ، وكل من كان
Second Chapter: On presenting other proofs derived from the agency of the First Principle. Know that the people can say: The maker and the agent are from the category of relation. For 'maker' is said in relation to the made, just as 'made' is said in relation to the maker, and the two relatives exist together. If the made exists potentially, then the maker is a maker potentially; and if the made exists actually, then the maker is a maker actually. And when this is established, we say: If the world were not eternal, then the made would not exist actually in eternity but potentially. And if it were so, then the maker would not be a maker actually in eternity, but potentially. Then when in the future the made exists, the maker becomes in the future a maker actually. So it is established: if the world were temporally originated, it would necessitate that it be said: the maker was a maker potentially, then became a maker actually. And everything that was potential and then becomes actual, its transition from potentiality to actuality and from non-existence to existence is not from its essence; otherwise it would always be actual. Rather, that transition must be because something else moved it from potentiality to actuality. So if the world were temporally originated, the maker would need something to transfer him from being a maker potentially to being a maker actually. And everyone who...
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents a new argument for the eternity of the world, based on the logical relationship between 'maker' (صانع) and 'made' (مصنوع). It begins by noting that these terms are relative; they exist together either potentially or actually. The author then assumes for reductio that the world is not eternal. In that case, in eternity (al-azal), the made (i.e., the world) does not exist actually, so the maker is only a maker potentially. When the world later comes into existence, the maker becomes actually a maker. This transition in the maker from potentiality to actuality requires an external cause, because nothing moves itself from potentiality to actuality (otherwise it would always be actual). Therefore, if the world were originated, the maker would need something else to transfer it from potential maker to actual maker, leading to an infinite regress or contradiction. Hence, the world must be eternal. The argument is incomplete in the text but the core reasoning is clear.

مصنوع
مقولة المضاف
قوة
فعل
أزل
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument implies that God's essence changes.
Correction: The argument only discusses a change in the relational attribute of 'maker', not in God's essence. The problem is that even a relational change would require an external cause, which is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion the author draws from the relational nature of maker and made?
A) The world must be eternal to avoid change in God.
B) The world was created ex nihilo.
C) The maker is always actual.
D) Potentiality and actuality are irrelevant to God.

Key Takeaway
The relational nature of maker and made forces the conclusion that the world must be eternal to prevent any change in God.

?

Two Objections to Divine Causality and Their Answers
Razi presents two objections regarding daily events and causal dependence, and answers them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two objections against Razi's proof that God is the origin of daily events, and how he answers them through the concept of successive conditions and the nature of causal relations.

Core Idea
God's causal role in daily events is preserved through successive conditions, not direct temporal breaks.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi anticipates two objections to his argument that God is the origin of all events. The first objection questions how God can be the source of daily occurrences like rain. The second objection argues that if a maker depends on the made, then God would be dependent on creation. Razi carefully responds to both, preserving divine independence and eternal causality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
Read the two objections presented at the beginning. Notice how each challenges the idea of God as the origin of daily events and the nature of causal dependence.
The first objection is about daily events, the second about the maker-made relation.
2
Examine the first answer
Study Razi's response to the first objection. He refers back to his earlier proof and explains that every event is conditioned by a prior event, without an ultimate first. The eternal cause remains, and temporal specification comes from successive conditions.
This answer preserves God's eternal causality while explaining temporal diversity.
3
Examine the second answer
Analyze Razi's answer to the second objection. He distinguishes between the essence of the cause producing the effect, and the subsequent relation of causality. The cause's essence is effective prior to the relation, so it does not depend on the effect.
The key is that causality is a relation that arises after the effect exists, not a condition for the cause's efficacy.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how both answers defend the independence and eternal nature of God's causality while accounting for the occurrence of new events. How do these responses strengthen Razi's proof?
Think about why the objections arise from a misunderstanding of causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : السؤال عليه من وجهين :
الأول : إن هذا يشكل بكون الله مبدأ للحوادث اليومية
الثاني : إنه إن كان كون الصانع صانعاً بالفعل ، يتوقف على كون المصنوع مصنوعاً بالفعل ، فحينئذ يكون الصانع محتاجاً إلى الوجود المصنوع .
والجواب عن الأول : ما تقدم ذكره في الحجة الأولى ، حيث ذكرنا : أن حدوث كل حادث ، مشروط بحدوث حادث قبله ، لا إلى أول ، فيكون المبدأ الموجب المؤثر ، وهو المبدأ الأول الأزلي القديم إلا أن التخصيص والحدوث، إنما حصل بسبب هذه الشرائط المتعاقبة
والجواب عن السؤال الثاني : إن ذات العلة ، موجدة لذاته المعلول ، ثم إذا حصل هاتان الذاتان حصلت بينهما إضافة العلية والمعلولية ، فذات العلة مؤثرة في ذات المعلول بالاتفاق ثم إذا وجدت ذات المعلول ، فحينئذ تكون هاتان الذاتان موجبتين للإضافتين المخصوصيتن ، أعني العلية والمعلولية . هذا تمام تقرير هذا الوجه
For it is said: The question against it is from two aspects:
First: This is problematic regarding God being the origin of daily occurring events.
Second: If the maker's being an actual maker depends on the thing made being actually made, then the maker would be in need of the made existence.
The answer to the first: What was previously mentioned in the first proof, where we stated that the occurrence of every event is conditioned by the occurrence of an event before it, without an ultimate beginning, so the necessitating effecting origin — which is the first eternal beginning — except that the specification and occurrence happen due to these successive conditions.
The answer to the second question: The essence of the cause brings into existence the essence of the effect. Then when these two essences exist, the relation of causality and effecthood obtains between them. So the essence of the cause is effective in the essence of the effect by agreement. Then when the essence of the effect exists, then these two essences necessitate the two specific relations, namely causality and effecthood. This is the full presentation of this aspect.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents two objections to the previous proof and their answers. The first objection: the proof seems to contradict the fact that God brings about new daily events (like rain, etc.). The answer: as mentioned earlier in the first proof, every new event is conditioned by a preceding event, without an ultimate first. So the eternal cause remains the same, and temporal specification comes from successive conditions. The second objection: if the maker's being actually maker depends on the thing being actually made, then the maker would need the made thing. The answer: the cause's essence produces the effect's essence. When both essences exist, the relation of causality arises. So the cause's essence is effective in the effect's essence, and the relation follows after the existence of both.

Correction: Razi explains through successive conditions that events are real and God's causality is eternal and unchanging.

Razi's Critique of the Additional Effectiveness Premise
Razi critiques the assumption that effectiveness is additional, leading to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why assuming that an agent's effectiveness is an additional attribute leads to an infinite regress, and how this critique undermines a key premise in the proof.

Core Idea
If effectiveness is added on, it never ends.

This passage presents a critical remark by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi on a widely used proof. The proof relies on the idea that an agent's effectiveness is a separate attribute from the agent and effect. Razi shows that this assumption leads to an infinite regress, rendering the premise unsound. This is a powerful logical critique that any student of kalam must grasp.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'واعلم' (And know) signals an important point.
2
Identify the key assumption
Find where Razi states the pivot of the proof: that effectiveness (مؤثرية) is an additional attribute (صفة زائدة). Underline this assumption.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the reasoning: additional attribute → depends on agent's essence → dependent things are contingent (ممكن لذاته) → contingent needs a cause → effectiveness requires another effectiveness → infinite regress.
Each step is built from a simple rule of kalam logic.
4
Understand why regress is impossible
Infinite regress (تسلسل) is considered rationally impossible (محال) because it would require an infinite chain of causes with no first cause. Reflect on why this is a problem.
5
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If the assumption leads to impossibility, what does that say about the proof that depends on it? This critique does not disprove the whole argument but shows the premise is unsound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن مدار هذه الحجة على أن مؤثرية المؤثر في الأثر : صفة زائدة على ذات المؤثر، وذات الأثر. وهذا مشكل لأنه لو كان الأمر كذلك، لكانت تلك المؤثرية صفة مفتقرة إلى ذات المؤثر . والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته ، والممكن لذاته محتاج إلى المؤثر ، فكانت مؤثرية المؤثر في حصول تلك المؤثرية زائدة . ولزم التسلسل ، وهو محال والله علم
And know that the pivot of this proof is that the effectiveness of the agent in the effect is an attribute additional to the essence of the agent and the essence of the effect. But this is problematic, because if it were so, that effectiveness would be an attribute dependent on the essence of the agent. Whatever is dependent on another is contingent in itself, and the contingent in itself needs an effect. So the effectiveness of the agent in the occurrence of that effectiveness would be additional, and infinite regress would follow, which is impossible. And God knows.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a critical remark on the entire proof. The author notes that the proof's basis is the claim that effectiveness is additional to the agent and the effect. But this is problematic: if effectiveness is additional, it is dependent on the agent's essence. Whatever depends on another is contingent (mumkin) in itself, and the contingent needs an effect. Thus the effectiveness of the agent in producing that effectiveness would itself need another effectiveness, leading to infinite regress. This shows the basis is unsound. The passage ends with a phrase indicating prudence: 'And God knows'.

Key Terms
ممكن لذاته
مفتقر إلى الغير
محتاج إلى المؤثر
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The assumption that effectiveness is an additional attribute is harmless and does not affect the proof.
Correction: Razi shows that this assumption leads to an infinite regress, proving it is rationally untenable.

Checkpoint

A) The attribute is eternal
B) The effect is eternal
C) Infinite regress of effectiveness
D) The cause is not needed

Key Takeaway
Razi's critique shows that treating effectiveness as an additional attribute leads to an infinite regress, thereby exposing a fatal flaw in the proof's basis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Razi's critique shows that adding a layer to explain something can create infinite problems. In your own life, where do you try to 'add' explanations or justifications that actually complicate your faith? How does this lesson push you to simplify your understanding and accept the primacy of Allah's essence without overcomplicating?
?
The passage ends with 'And God knows,' indicating humility before divine knowledge. In what areas of your belief are you unwilling to leave room for mystery or to submit to the limits of human reasoning? Can you identify a specific belief where you need to stop arguing from your own assumptions and instead trust that God's nature is beyond full comprehension?

The Regress Problem in Agency as an Extra Attribute
Razi identifies a problematic assumption in a proof and warns of infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why assuming that agency (effectivity) is an attribute separate from the agent's essence leads to an impossible infinite regress.

Core Idea
If agency were a separate attribute, it would need another agency to exist, creating an endless chain.

Razi identifies a critical assumption underlying a particular proof: that the effectivity of an agent in producing an effect is an additional attribute, distinct from both the agent and the effect. He then shows that this assumption is problematic because it leads to an infinite regress. This lesson explores that logical difficulty and its implications for the proof's validity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Notice how Razi presents the assumption and then challenges it.
The passage is concise; each phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the key assumption
The pivot of the proof is that 'effectivity' (مؤثرية) is an additional attribute over and above the essences of the agent and effect. Identify this assumption in the text.
The term 'مؤثرية' means 'effectivity' or 'agent-causality'.
3
Follow the regress argument
If effectivity is a separate attribute, it depends on the agent's essence, making it contingent. As contingent, it requires its own effectivity, and so on. Trace this chain back to see the regress.
The argument uses the kalam principle: whatever depends on another is contingent (ممكن).
4
Understand the conclusion
The regress is impossible (محال), so the original assumption must be false. Razi does not offer an alternative here but points out the flaw.
This does not disprove that agency is an attribute; it only shows a problem with that particular proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن مدار هذه الحجة على أن مؤثرية المؤثر في الأثر: صفة زائدة على ذات المؤثر وذات الأثر. وهذا مشكل لأنه لو كان الأمر كذلك، لكانت تلك المؤثرية صفة مفتقرة إلى ذات المؤثر. والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته، والممكن لذاته محتاج إلى المؤثر، فكانت مؤثرية المؤثر في حصول تلك المؤثرية زائدة. ولزم التسلسل، وهو محال. والله علم.
Know that the pivot of this proof is that the effectivity of the agent in the effect is an additional attribute to the essence of the agent and the essence of the effect. And this is problematic, because if it were so, then that effectivity would be an attribute in need of the essence of the agent. And what is need of another is contingent in itself, and what is contingent in itself is in need of an effect, so the effectivity of the agent in obtaining that effectivity would be additional. And infinite regress would follow, which is impossible. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage identifies a problematic assumption in the proof: that the effectivity (مؤثرية) is an attribute separate from both the agent's essence and the effect's essence. If that were the case, then that attribute would itself depend on the agent's essence, making it contingent and thus in need of another effectivity to bring it about, leading to an infinite regress. The author points out this difficulty, implying that the proof may need refinement.

Correction: The passage focuses on a logical flaw in a specific proof about agency being a separate attribute. It does not address the eternity of the world or divine attributes directly; it is a critique of the proof's structure.

Key Takeaway
Assuming that agency is an attribute separate from the agent's essence leads to an impossible infinite regress, revealing a flaw in the proof that relies on that assumption.

?
The danger here is an infinite regress—a chain with no foundation. In your own life, are there patterns of behavior or thought that keep going without ever being anchored in truth? What would it take to stop that cycle and ground yourself in what is certain?
?

The Three Possibilities of the Act of Causing
A proof analyzing the act of causing into three possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the act of causing (ijad) cannot be identical to the agent or the effect, and why a third distinct entity is necessary.

Core Idea
The act of causing must be distinct from both the agent and the effect.

This passage presents a refined expression of a proof against the temporality of the world. It dissects the act of causing into three logical possibilities, eliminating two of them to arrive at the necessary conclusion. Understanding this analysis is crucial for grasping the broader argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and grasp the scenario
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. The passage sets up a conditional: if the world is temporal, it must be caused. Then it asks what the act of causing is.
Notice the three options presented.
2
Analyze the first two possibilities
Identify the reasoning against each: if causing is the agent's essence, then eternal essence implies eternal effect. If causing is the effect itself, then the world is self-caused.
Both lead to contradictions with the claim of temporality.
3
Understand why the third possibility remains
The only remaining option is that causing is a third thing distinct from both. This sets up the next part of the proof.
Pay attention to how the argument forces this conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يعبر عن هذه الحجة بعبارة أخرى : فيقال : لو كان العالم حادثاً ، لامتنع أن يدخل في الوجود إلا بإيجاد الفاعل . فنقول : إيجاد الفاعل : إما أن يكون عبارة عن ذات الفاعل، أو عن ذات المفعول ، أو عن أمر ثالث . فإن كان الأول ، لزم من دوام ذات الفاعل دوام ذات المفعول . وإن كان الثاني كان معناه : أن العالم إنما وجد بنفسه . لأنا لما قلنا : إن العالم وجد بإيجاد الفاعل . ثم قلنا : إن إيجاد الفاعل للعالم هو نفس العالم ، كان معنى هذا الكلام : أن العالم وجد بنفسه ، لكن كونه كذلك ، يمنع من إسناده إلى الفاعل ، وذلك محال . فبقي الثالث . فنقول : لا شك أن الموجدية صفة لذات الفاعل ، فهي إن كانت حادثة افتقرت إلى إيجاد آخر . وهو محال . وإن كانت قديمة، لزم من قدمها قدم المفعول، لأن كون الموجد موجداً ، حال بقاء المفعول على عدمه الأصلي : محال . وبالله التوفيق
And this proof is sometimes expressed in other words: it is said: If the world were temporal, it would be impossible for it to come into existence except through the act of causing by the agent. So we say: The act of causing by the agent: either it is identical to the essence of the agent, or to the essence of the effect, or to a third thing. If it is the first, then from the permanence of the essence of the agent would follow the permanence of the essence of the effect. And if it is the second, its meaning would be that the world came into existence by itself. Because when we said: The world came into existence through the act of causing by the agent. Then we said: The act of causing by the agent for the world is the world itself, the meaning of this statement would be: The world came into existence by itself, but its being so prevents its attribution to the agent, and that is impossible. So the third remains. We say: There is no doubt that the attribute of being a cause is an attribute of the essence of the agent. So if it were temporal, it would need another act of causing. And that is impossible. And if it were eternal, from its eternity would follow the eternity of the effect, because the fact that the cause is a cause while the effect remains in its original non-existence is impossible. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a proof (a second expression of an earlier argument) that refutes the claim that the world is created in time (hadith). It begins by restating the argument: if the world were temporal, it would need an efficient cause (إيجاد الفاعل). The discussion then analyzes the concept of 'act of causing' (إيجاد) into three possibilities: it is either identical to the essence of the agent (ذات الفاعل), or identical to the essence of the effect (ذات المفعول), or something separate. The first possibility would mean that since the agent's essence is eternal, the effect would also be eternal—contradicting the claim of temporality. The second possibility equates the act of causing with the world itself, which would imply that the world brought itself into existence, making the agent superfluous—an impossibility. Therefore, only the third possibility remains: the act of causing is something distinct from both. The argument then examines the attribute of being a cause (الموجدية) which belongs to the agent. If this attribute were temporal, it would require another act of causing to bring it about, leading to an infinite regress—impossible. If it were eternal, then the effect would also be eternal, because a cause cannot be a cause while the effect remains non-existent—also impossible. Thus, all possibilities lead to absurdity, proving that the world cannot be temporal and must be eternal. The phrase 'And with God is success' ends the proof.

Key Terms
إيجاد الفاعل
ذات الفاعل
ذات المفعول
أمر ثالث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The act of causing (ijad) is the same as the agent's essence or the effect's essence.
Correction: The proof shows both identifications lead to contradictions, so ijad must be a third distinct thing.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would follow if the act of causing were identical to the essence of the agent?
A) The effect would be temporal.
B) The effect would be eternal.
C) The agent would be unnecessary.
D) The act of causing would be temporal.

Key Takeaway
The act of causing must be a distinct entity from both the agent and the effect, otherwise contradictions arise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen that the act of causing must be distinct from both the agent and the effect. Do you truly live as though every good thing in your life comes through a distinct act of Allah, or do you sometimes feel that things happen automatically? How would your gratitude change if you were always aware of that distinct act of causing?
?
If the act of causing were identical to Allah's essence, then the world would be eternal. This is impossible. Does your heart ever assume that Allah's actions are just 'automatic' because He is perfect? How does this proof teach you to distinguish between who Allah is and what He does, and why must you maintain that distinction?
?
The proof eliminates the possibility that the world caused itself. Are there areas in your life where you secretly act as if you are the cause of your own success or failure, forgetting that the very act of causing is a separate thing from you? What would it mean for you to sincerely attribute every effect to Allah's distinct causing act?

The Attribute of Being a Cause and Its Implications
Analyzing the attribute of being a cause to refute temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of being a cause (mujidiyya) cannot be temporal or eternal without leading to contradiction.

Core Idea
The attribute of being a cause must be eternal, but that forces the effect to be eternal too.

This second part of the proof focuses on the attribute of being a cause, which characterizes the agent. It examines whether this attribute itself is temporal or eternal, and shows both options lead to absurdity. This seals the argument against the temporality of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about 'mujidiyya'
Read the Arabic and translation for this section. Note that it starts by affirming that the attribute of being a cause is a property of the agent's essence.
This is the third thing from the previous lesson.
2
Consider the first scenario: temporal
If the attribute is temporal, it would require another act of causing to bring it about, leading to an infinite regress.
This is impossible because it would never end.
3
Consider the second scenario: eternal
If the attribute is eternal, then the effect must also be eternal, because a cause cannot be a cause while the effect remains non-existent.
Thus the world would be eternal, contradicting the claim of temporality.
4
Understand the overall conclusion
Both possibilities lead to impossibility, so the original claim that the world is temporal cannot be true. The proof concludes with 'And with God is success.'
This is a standard pious ending.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It begins by restating the argument: if the world were temporal, it would need an efficient cause (إيجاد الفاعل). The discussion then analyzes the concept of 'act of causing' (إيجاد) into three possibilities: it is either identical to the essence of the agent (ذات الفاعل), or identical to the essence of the effect (ذات المفعول), or something separate. The first possibility would mean that since the agent's essence is eternal, the effect would also be eternal—contradicting the claim of temporality. The second possibility equates the act of causing with the world itself, which would imply that the world brought itself into existence, making the agent superfluous—an impossibility. Therefore, only the third possibility remains: the act of causing is something distinct from both. The argument then examines the attribute of being a cause (الموجدية) which belongs to the agent. If this attribute were temporal, it would require another act of causing to bring it about, leading to an infinite regress—impossible. If it were eternal, then the effect would also be eternal, because a cause cannot be a cause while the effect remains non-existent—also impossible. Thus, all possibilities lead to absurdity, proving that the world cannot be temporal and must be eternal. The phrase 'And with God is success' ends the proof.

Key Terms
الموجدية
صفة
ذات الفاعل
حادثة
قديمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The attribute of being a cause is the same as the act of causing itself.
Correction: The passage distinguishes them: the act of causing is a third thing, and the attribute of being a cause is a property of the agent.

Checkpoint
What would happen if the attribute of being a cause (mujidiyya) were eternal?
A) It would need another cause.
B) The effect would be eternal.
C) The attribute would be temporal.
D) The agent would be unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
The attribute of being a cause cannot be temporal or eternal without contradiction, so the world cannot be temporal.

Does your prayer life reflect a reliance on this eternal, unchanging power, or do you sometimes act as if God's help is only available at certain moments? What will you do today to consciously depend on this eternal attribute?
?
If the effect (your existence) must be eternal if the cause is eternal, then your dependence on God is not a one-time event but a continual reality. How does this change the way you view your own creation and ongoing existence? Will you live today more aware of your perpetual need for Him?
?
This argument forces you to reject the idea that God 'began' creating at some point. How does your heart respond to the thought that God's creative power has always been active? Are you comfortable with a God whose actions are not bound by time? If not, what assumption about time and God are you holding onto that you need to surrender?

Fourth Proof: Wise Agent Requires Cause for Change
Sanusi presents the fourth proof against the world's temporality: a wise agent's change requires an external cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof for the world's eternity, specifically why a wise agent cannot transition from inaction to action without an external cause.

Core Idea
A wise agent's change demands an external reason; without it, change is impossible or foolish.

Sanusi now turns to a proof rooted in the nature of a wise agent. He argues that if the world were temporal, God would have changed from non-action to action without cause—something impossible for a wise being. This lesson unpacks the first part of that proof, establishing the principle using a vivid human example.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the opening lines where Sanusi states the fourth proof. Notice how he frames it as a conditional: if the world were temporal, then God would have changed from non-acting to acting, which is impossible.
2
Identify the two مقامان (points)
Find where Sanusi says 'we need to establish two points'. He outlines the first point but the passage ends before the second. Focus on the first point: the wise agent cannot change without an external cause.
The second point is not covered in this lesson.
3
Study the human example
Sanusi illustrates with a man sitting for years then suddenly acting. Trace the reasoning: why would such a change be either impossible or foolish if no cause occurs?
This example clarifies the principle for human agents, then applies by analogy to God.
4
Connect to divine attribute
Reflect on how this proof defends God's wisdom. If God changed without cause, He would be like the foolish man—but God is free from that. Therefore, God must always have been acting, implying the world's eternity.
This is the core conclusion of the proof.

The fourth proof: They said: If the world were temporal, then the first agent would never cease to be non-acting, and everything that was not an agent becomes an agent in a wise manner only when something changes—that change not being from him. That is impossible with respect to God. So it must be impossible that He becomes an agent after He had not been an agent. And since this is false, we know that He was an agent without ceasing. So here we need to establish two points: The first point: The wise agent is prevented from being an agent after having been non-acting except when something changes—that change not being from him. The proof of it: If we suppose a man who was sitting with us for a long time by his choice, then he stood up from us by his choice, this change only occurred because a change occurred in some state not by his choice, such as the approach of nightfall, so the man stood up from that place for that reason. Or he remembered something he had forgotten, or news reached him of which he was unaware. If no happening at all occurs to transfer this man from the first opinion to its opposite, it is impossible that his state changes in action and inaction. We say: This is either impossible, or if not impossible, it is foolishness. For if a man was sitting in his house for seventy years, not moving, not engaging in any interest or improvement of his condition, then after that he springs up suddenly with great seriousness and intense effort, and begins actions and organizing important matters, and then he is asked: 'What is the reason that necessitated the transition from the previous leisure to this complete occupation?'—if he does not mention any reason at all, that is either impossible in itself or pure frivolity and foolishness. So it is established by this that the free, wise agent does not transition from inaction to action except due to a changing cause, and that change must not be from
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof (الحجة الرابعة) against the doctrine that the world is temporal. The proof is based on the nature of a wise agent. The argument states that if the world were temporal, God (the First Agent) would have been non-acting for an eternity and then became acting. But a wise agent cannot change from non-action to action without some cause external to his own choice. That would be impossible for God. Therefore, God must have always been acting, implying the world must be eternal. The proof requires establishing two points (مقامان). The first point, which is the focus of this passage, is that a wise agent cannot change from non-agent to agent without a cause that is not from him. The proof is illustrated with a human example: a man sitting by choice for a long time only stands up due to an external cause (like nightfall, memory, news). If no such cause exists, a sudden change without reason is either impossible or pure foolishness. Thus, for God, who is supremely wise, such a change without cause is impossible. The passage ends mid-sentence at the end of the page, indicating that the second point would follow.

الترك
الفعل
التغير
السفه
المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can decide to create the world arbitrarily at any moment without any reason or cause.
Correction: The proof shows that a wise agent cannot change from inaction to action without an external cause; for God, who is perfectly wise, such an arbitrary change would be impossible or imply foolishness.

Checkpoint

A) The agent must will the change.
B) A change must occur that is not from the agent.
C) The agent must be compelled by a superior force.
D) No change is necessary; the agent can act arbitrarily.

Key Takeaway
A wise agent's transition from non-action to action is impossible without an external cause; otherwise, it is either impossible or foolish.

?

Think of a time you changed your mind about something important without a real reason. Did that feel wise? How does the principle that change requires an external cause expose the foolishness in your own decisions?
?
If God never acts without a reason, what does that imply about your trust in His plan, even when you don't see the reason? Are you willing to accept His wisdom over your own understanding?

The Fourth Proof: An Introduction
Al-Razi states the fourth proof and announces two stations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the fourth proof and the opponents' claim that if the world were created, God would have been inactive in eternity.

Core Idea
A wise agent cannot start acting without an external change.

This lesson introduces the fourth proof from the opponents. They argue that if the world were created (hadith), then God would have been non-agent in eternity and only became active later, which is impossible for a wise agent unless a change occurs. Since that change is impossible for God, the world must be eternal. We'll see the overall structure and the two stations needed to refute this.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Start with the opponents' claim that if the world is created, then the first agent would have been non-agent for eternity and then became an agent. Notice the key phrase 'lam yazal' (never ceased).
This is the core objection that the proof aims to refute.
2
Identify the conclusion
The opponents conclude that since it is impossible for God to change due to an external factor, He must have been an agent without beginning (lam yazal).
3
Note the two stations
The author says two 'maqams' (stations) need to be established. These will be the focus of the next lessons.
This sets up the structure of the proof.
